```python
NPES_ABLATION = [
    {"name": "npes_sink_only",        "lambda_s": 0.35, "sigma_d": 3.0, "beta": 0.0, "gamma": 0.0},
    {"name": "npes_bg_gated",         "lambda_s": 0.35, "sigma_d": 3.0, "beta": 2.0, "gamma": 0.0},
    {"name": "npes_conf_gated",       "lambda_s": 0.35, "sigma_d": 3.0, "beta": 2.0, "gamma": 0.15},
    {"name": "npes_tuned_moderate",   "lambda_s": 0.45, "sigma_d": 2.5, "beta": 2.0, "gamma": 0.15},
    {"name": "npes_tuned_aggressive", "lambda_s": 0.55, "sigma_d": 2.0, "beta": 1.5, "gamma": 0.10},
]
```

## Relationship to Known Methods

NPES draws on two classical ideas, combined in a novel way specific to the Alpha Fix pipeline:

- **Narrow-band level-set methods** use signed distance fields and restrict computation to a band around the zero level set. NPES uses signed distance similarly but as a gating signal for a multiplicative alpha correction rather than as a contour evolution PDE.[8][7]
- **Anisotropic diffusion (Perona-Malik)** modulates diffusion strength based on local image gradients. NPES's background-distance gate \(G(p)\) serves an analogous role — modulating correction strength based on color evidence — but operates as a one-shot multiplicative correction rather than an iterative diffusion process.[9][10]

The specific combination — one-sided signed-distance gating × background-color evidence × confidence floor × multiplicative alpha sink — is novel to this pipeline and does not correspond to a named method in the matting or level-set literature.

# NPES: Normal-Projected Exterior Sink — Alpha Fix Experiment Design

## 1. Method Name

**Normal-Projected Exterior Sink (NPES)**

The name reflects the three core ideas: (a) boundary-normal projection to classify interior vs. exterior, (b) a monotone "sink" force that pulls alpha toward zero, and (c) restriction to the exterior side only.

## 2. Diagnosis: Why v2 Still Bleeds

The v2 branch achieves excellent chatter (0.002371) and improved temporal jitter (0.026415) via two mechanisms: the CNVM confidence map suppresses noisy alpha flicker, and the adaptive temporal EMA stabilizes alpha values across frames. Both mechanisms are **side-agnostic** — they smooth equally toward the interior (foreground) and exterior (background) sides of the boundary.

The root cause of residual bleed is a **symmetric stabilization bias**:

- The temporal EMA anchors alpha toward its recent running average. If a pixel on the exterior side of the boundary briefly receives \(\alpha > 0\) from the refinement step (due to compression artifacts, LAB distance ambiguity, or soft-edge overshoot), the EMA **locks that nonzero value in** rather than letting it decay to zero.
- The CNVM confidence map gates the update strength but does not know whether the pixel is on the foreground or background side. A pixel at signed distance \(d = +2\text{px}\) (exterior) with moderate confidence gets the same treatment as one at \(d = -2\text{px}\) (interior).
- The anchor \(\alpha_0\) is hard/binary, so the soft transition zone created by the refinement inherently extends outward. Nothing in the current pipeline applies a **directional correction** that specifically targets this outward overshoot.

In short: v2 is excellent at temporal consistency but has no mechanism to distinguish "stable nonzero alpha that belongs to the foreground" from "stable nonzero alpha that leaked into the background." NPES provides that mechanism.

## 3. Mathematical Formulation

### Symbol Definitions

| Symbol | Definition |
|--------|-----------|
| \(\alpha(p)\) | Current refined alpha at pixel \(p\) |
| \(\alpha_0(p)\) | Anchor alpha (hard binary mask from initial segmentation) |
| \(d(p)\) | Signed distance from \(p\) to the \(\alpha_0 = 0.5\) boundary; positive = exterior (background side), negative = interior (foreground side) |
| \(\hat{n}(p)\) | Outward unit boundary normal at the nearest boundary point to \(p\), computed from \(\nabla d\) |
| \(C(p)\) | CNVM confidence map value at \(p\), in \([0, 1]\) |
| \(b_{\text{LAB}}(p)\) | Normalized LAB Mahalanobis distance to background model at \(p\), in \([0, 1]\) |
| \(\alpha_{\text{prev}}(p)\) | Temporally warped previous-frame alpha at \(p\) |
| \(R\) | Narrow band half-width in pixels |
| \(\lambda_s\) | Sink strength (main tuning knob) |
| \(\sigma_d\) | Signed-distance falloff scale (pixels) |
| \(\beta\) | Background-distance gating exponent |
| \(\gamma\) | Confidence floor for sink activation |

### The Exterior Projection

For each pixel \(p\) in the narrow band, compute the **normal-projected exterior coordinate**:

\[ e(p) = \max\bigl(0,\; d(p)\bigr) \]

This is simply the positive part of the signed distance — zero for interior/boundary pixels, positive for exterior pixels. This is the fundamental asymmetry: \(e(p) = 0\) on the foreground side, so the sink force vanishes there exactly.[1]

### The Sink Potential

Define a smooth, monotone sink potential that maps each exterior pixel's alpha toward zero with strength proportional to how far exterior it is:

\[ S(p) = 1 - \exp\!\Bigl(-\frac{e(p)^2}{2\,\sigma_d^2}\Bigr) \]

This is a Gaussian-complement profile: \(S = 0\) at the boundary (\(e = 0\)), rising smoothly toward 1 as \(e\) increases. The scale \(\sigma_d\) controls how quickly the sink engages — at \(e = \sigma_d\), \(S \approx 0.39\); at \(e = 2\sigma_d\), \(S \approx 0.86\).[2]

### The Background-Distance Gate

To avoid clamping legitimate foreground detail that happens to fall at positive signed distance (e.g., hair wisps extending beyond the hard anchor), gate the sink by how "background-like" the pixel's color is:

\[ G(p) = \bigl(1 - b_{\text{LAB}}(p)\bigr)^\beta \]

When \(b_{\text{LAB}}\) is low (pixel color is close to background), \(G\) is high, and the sink fires strongly. When \(b_{\text{LAB}}\) is high (pixel color differs from background — likely real foreground), \(G\) is low, protecting that pixel. The exponent \(\beta\) controls sensitivity.[3]

### The Confidence Gate

Only apply the sink where confidence is not extremely low (which would indicate genuine ambiguity that shouldn't be forced):

\[ W_C(p) = \text{clamp}\!\Bigl(\frac{C(p) - \gamma}{1 - \gamma},\; 0,\; 1\Bigr) \]

This soft-thresholds the confidence at floor \(\gamma\). Below \(\gamma\), the sink is fully suppressed — preserving the anti-chatter smoothing in ambiguous white-on-white regions.[4]

### The NPES Correction

The complete correction applied to alpha:

\[ \Delta\alpha_{\text{NPES}}(p) = -\lambda_s \cdot S(p) \cdot G(p) \cdot W_C(p) \cdot \alpha(p) \]

[5]

The corrected alpha:

\[ \alpha'(p) = \text{clamp}\bigl(\alpha(p) + \Delta\alpha_{\text{NPES}}(p),\; 0,\; 1\bigr) \]

[6]

Key properties of this correction:

- **Always non-positive**: \(\Delta\alpha_{\text{NPES}} \leq 0\). It can only reduce alpha, never increase it. This is a true sink.
- **Proportional to current alpha**: The \(\cdot\,\alpha(p)\) factor means the correction is multiplicative — it removes a fraction of the existing alpha rather than subtracting a fixed amount. This avoids creating negative alpha or hard clipping artifacts.
- **Zero on the interior**: When \(e(p) = 0\) (foreground side), \(S(p) = 0\), so \(\Delta\alpha = 0\). No foreground thinning.
- **Zero at the boundary**: The Gaussian-complement ensures a smooth onset, not a hard threshold.

## 4. Core Anti-Bleed Mechanism

The anti-bleed mechanism works through a **directional asymmetry chain**:

1. **Signed distance** partitions the narrow band into interior (\(d < 0\)) and exterior (\(d > 0\)).
2. **The max(0, ·) clamp** ensures the sink potential is identically zero for all interior pixels.
3. **The Gaussian-complement profile** provides a smooth, convex ramp that increases correction strength further from the boundary — exactly where bleed is worst.
4. **The background-distance gate** adds a second safety net: even on the exterior side, if a pixel's LAB color looks like foreground, the gate suppresses the sink.
5. **The multiplicative form** (\(\cdot\,\alpha\)) ensures that pixels with small residual alpha get tiny corrections (no artifacts), while pixels with large erroneous alpha get proportionally larger corrections (effective cleanup).

This is conceptually related to one-sided velocity fields in narrow-band level-set methods, where the propagation speed can be restricted to one direction of the signed distance field. The key difference is that NPES operates as a post-hoc correction on an existing alpha field rather than evolving a contour, and the "velocity" is replaced by a multiplicative alpha reduction gated by color and confidence.[7]

## 5. Why It Preserves Low Chatter

Chatter arises from frame-to-frame alpha fluctuations in ambiguous boundary regions. NPES preserves the v2 anti-chatter gains for three reasons:

1. **Confidence gating**: The \(W_C\) term suppresses the sink in low-confidence regions (below floor \(\gamma\)). These are exactly the white-on-white ambiguous zones where chatter originates. The sink simply does not fire there.
2. **Deterministic correction**: \(\Delta\alpha_{\text{NPES}}\) is a deterministic function of \(d\), \(b_{\text{LAB}}\), \(C\), and the current \(\alpha\). It does not introduce any new stochastic or threshold-based switching that could cause flickering.
3. **Monotone sink**: The correction is always non-positive and proportional to \(\alpha\). It pulls alpha toward zero monotonically. Once the temporal EMA stabilizes a value, NPES either leaves it alone (interior/ambiguous) or reduces it smoothly (exterior/background-like). It never oscillates.

## 6. Why It Preserves Low Temporal Jitter

Temporal jitter is alpha variation between consecutive frames after optical-flow warping. NPES preserves the v2 jitter improvement because:

1. **Applied after temporal EMA**: NPES is inserted after the temporal EMA step, so it operates on already-stabilized alpha values. It does not interfere with the EMA's smoothing.
2. **Smooth spatial profile**: The Gaussian-complement sink potential is \(C^\infty\) smooth. Small frame-to-frame variations in the signed distance or boundary position produce proportionally small changes in the correction, rather than abrupt jumps.
3. **Background-distance stability**: The LAB background distance \(b_{\text{LAB}}\) is relatively stable across frames (it depends on the static background model, not the current frame's noise). This means the gate \(G(p)\) is temporally consistent.
4. **No iterative convergence issue**: NPES is a single-pass closed-form correction, not an iterative solver that might converge to different fixed points on different frames.

## 7. Solver / Update Strategy

NPES is a **single-pass, closed-form local correction**. No iterations or variational solve required.

### Compute steps (per frame)

```
Step 1: Compute signed distance field d(p) from alpha0 boundary.
         → cv2.distanceTransform on alpha0 > 0.5 and its complement.
         → Combine with sign: d = d_interior * (-1) + d_exterior * (+1).
         Cost: O(N) via OpenCV distance transform.

Step 2: Compute exterior projection e(p) = max(0, d(p)).
         → Single numpy clip.

Step 3: Compute sink potential S(p) = 1 - exp(-e^2 / (2 * sigma_d^2)).
         → Elementwise numpy.

Step 4: Compute background gate G(p) = (1 - b_LAB)^beta.
         → b_LAB already exists in pipeline. Elementwise numpy.

Step 5: Compute confidence gate W_C(p) = clamp((C - gamma) / (1 - gamma), 0, 1).
         → C already exists in pipeline. Elementwise numpy.

Step 6: Compute correction: delta = -lambda_s * S * G * W_C * alpha.
         → Elementwise multiply.

Step 7: Apply: alpha_corrected = clamp(alpha + delta, 0, 1).
```

Total cost: one distance transform + five elementwise operations. Fully streaming-friendly. No dependency on future frames.

## 8. Pseudocode

```python
def apply_npes(alpha, alpha0, b_lab, confidence,
               lambda_s=0.45, sigma_d=3.0, beta=2.0, gamma=0.15):
    """
    Normal-Projected Exterior Sink.
    
    Args:
        alpha:      (H, W) float32, current refined alpha in [0, 1]
        alpha0:     (H, W) float32, anchor binary mask
        b_lab:      (H, W) float32, normalized LAB background distance in [0, 1]
        confidence: (H, W) float32, CNVM confidence map in [0, 1]
        lambda_s:   sink strength
        sigma_d:    signed-distance falloff scale in pixels
        beta:       background-distance gating exponent
        gamma:      confidence floor for sink activation
    
    Returns:
        alpha_corrected: (H, W) float32, corrected alpha
    """
    # Step 1: Signed distance field
    mask_fg = (alpha0 > 0.5).astype(np.uint8)
    mask_bg = 1 - mask_fg
    
    d_ext = cv2.distanceTransform(mask_fg, cv2.DIST_L2, 5).astype(np.float32)
    # d_ext is distance from bg pixels to nearest fg pixel — BUT we want
    # distance from each pixel to the boundary.
    # Actually: distanceTransform(mask_bg) gives distance of fg pixels to boundary
    # distanceTransform(mask_fg) gives distance of bg pixels to boundary
    # We want: positive outside fg, negative inside fg
    
    d_inside = cv2.distanceTransform(mask_fg, cv2.DIST_L2, 5).astype(np.float32)
    d_outside = cv2.distanceTransform(mask_bg, cv2.DIST_L2, 5).astype(np.float32)
    
    # signed distance: positive = exterior (background side)
    signed_d = np.where(mask_fg > 0, -d_outside, d_inside)
    
    # Step 2: Exterior projection (positive part only)
    e = np.maximum(signed_d, 0.0)
    
    # Step 3: Sink potential (Gaussian complement)
    S = 1.0 - np.exp(-(e ** 2) / (2.0 * sigma_d ** 2))
    
    # Step 4: Background-distance gate
    G = np.power(np.clip(1.0 - b_lab, 0.0, 1.0), beta)
    
    # Step 5: Confidence gate
    if gamma < 1.0:
        W_C = np.clip((confidence - gamma) / (1.0 - gamma), 0.0, 1.0)
    else:
        W_C = np.ones_like(confidence)
    
    # Step 6: Compute correction
    delta = -lambda_s * S * G * W_C * alpha
    
    # Step 7: Apply
    alpha_corrected = np.clip(alpha + delta, 0.0, 1.0)
    
    return alpha_corrected
```

### Narrow-Band Optimization

For performance, restrict computation to the narrow band mask:

```python
def apply_npes_banded(alpha, alpha0, b_lab, confidence, band_mask,
                      lambda_s=0.45, sigma_d=3.0, beta=2.0, gamma=0.15):
    """Narrow-band variant — only computes within band_mask."""
    # Signed distance still computed full-frame (cheap via OpenCV)
    mask_fg = (alpha0 > 0.5).astype(np.uint8)
    mask_bg = 1 - mask_fg
    d_inside = cv2.distanceTransform(mask_fg, cv2.DIST_L2, 5).astype(np.float32)
    d_outside = cv2.distanceTransform(mask_bg, cv2.DIST_L2, 5).astype(np.float32)
    signed_d = np.where(mask_fg > 0, -d_outside, d_inside)
    
    # Only process band pixels
    bp = band_mask > 0
    e_band = np.maximum(signed_d[bp], 0.0)
    S_band = 1.0 - np.exp(-(e_band ** 2) / (2.0 * sigma_d ** 2))
    G_band = np.power(np.clip(1.0 - b_lab[bp], 0.0, 1.0), beta)
    W_band = np.clip((confidence[bp] - gamma) / max(1.0 - gamma, 1e-8), 0.0, 1.0)
    
    delta_band = -lambda_s * S_band * G_band * W_band * alpha[bp]
    
    result = alpha.copy()
    result[bp] = np.clip(alpha[bp] + delta_band, 0.0, 1.0)
    return result
```

## 9. Insertion Points in Current Pipeline

The NPES correction inserts as a single new step in the existing pipeline:

```
[existing] confidence map computation       ← provides C(p)
[existing] narrow band computation          ← provides band_mask
[existing] alpha update / refinement        ← produces refined alpha
[existing] temporal EMA over warped alpha   ← stabilizes alpha
                                            
  ──► [NEW] NPES exterior sink correction   ← INSERT HERE
                                            
[existing] edge cleanup / despill           ← final polish
[existing] optional edge decontamination
```

### Why this insertion point:

- **After temporal EMA**: NPES operates on the EMA-stabilized alpha, so it does not fight the temporal smoother. The EMA has already done its job; NPES only removes the exterior-side residual that the EMA incorrectly preserved.
- **Before edge cleanup**: Edge cleanup (morphological operations, despill) can then operate on the already-corrected alpha. This prevents edge cleanup from fighting bleed that NPES has already removed.
- **After alpha update**: The refinement step produces the soft transition zone. NPES trims only its exterior tail, leaving the interior and boundary zone intact.

### Integration with existing signals:

| Signal | Source | Used by NPES as |
|--------|--------|-----------------|
| `confidence` | CNVM confidence map | Confidence gate \(W_C\) |
| `band_mask` | Narrow band computation | Spatial restriction |
| `alpha` | After temporal EMA step | Input alpha to correct |
| `alpha0` | Anchor alpha (frame-1 based) | Signed distance source |
| `b_lab` | LAB Mahalanobis distance | Background-distance gate \(G\) |

No new signals need to be computed. All inputs already exist in the pipeline.

## 10. Five-Pass Ablation Plan

Each pass enables one more component of NPES. This isolates the effect of each term.

| Pass | Name | \(\lambda_s\) | \(\sigma_d\) | \(\beta\) | \(\gamma\) | Description |
|------|------|-------------|-------------|---------|---------|-------------|
| P1 | `npes_sink_only` | 0.35 | 3.0 | 0.0 | 0.0 | Sink potential only. \(\beta=0 \Rightarrow G=1\), \(\gamma=0 \Rightarrow W_C=1\). Pure exterior signed-distance clamping. Measures raw anti-bleed effect and any foreground thinning. |
| P2 | `npes_bg_gated` | 0.35 | 3.0 | 2.0 | 0.0 | Add background-distance gate. \(G = (1-b_{\text{LAB}})^2\). Should recover foreground detail at exterior positions where color differs from background. |
| P3 | `npes_conf_gated` | 0.35 | 3.0 | 2.0 | 0.15 | Add confidence gate. Should preserve anti-chatter in ambiguous zones. |
| P4 | `npes_tuned_moderate` | 0.45 | 2.5 | 2.0 | 0.15 | Increase sink strength and tighten falloff. More aggressive bleed removal. Check for thinning. |
| P5 | `npes_tuned_aggressive` | 0.55 | 2.0 | 1.5 | 0.10 | Push parameters harder. Lower \(\beta\) = less foreground protection. Lower \(\gamma\) = sink fires in more ambiguous zones. This is the "how far can we go" test. |

### Expected outcomes:

- **P1**: Strong bleed reduction, possible foreground thinning at hair/detail, possible chatter increase (no confidence gate).
- **P2**: Similar bleed reduction, less thinning (background gate protects foreground-colored pixels).
- **P3**: Similar bleed + thinning as P2, but chatter should return to v2-level low (confidence gate active).
- **P4**: Better bleed than P3 due to stronger \(\lambda_s\) and tighter \(\sigma_d\). This is the expected sweet spot.
- **P5**: Maximum bleed reduction. If thinning or chatter regress, this tells us the parameter limits.

### Concrete metric targets:

| Metric | P3 target | P4 target | P5 limit |
|--------|-----------|-----------|----------|
| border_bleed | ≤ 0.0070 | ≤ 0.0065 | ≤ 0.0060 |
| chatter | ≤ 0.0035 | ≤ 0.0035 | ≤ 0.0050 |
| temporal_jitter | ≤ 0.0270 | ≤ 0.0270 | ≤ 0.0280 |
| mean_alpha | ≥ 0.435 | ≥ 0.430 | ≥ 0.425 |

## 11. Automatic Failure Metrics

### Exterior Bleed Detection

Already measured as `border_bleed` in the existing harness. Additionally, define a **directional bleed metric** for more granular diagnosis:

```python
def measure_exterior_bleed(alpha, alpha0, threshold=0.01):
    """Fraction of exterior-side narrow-band pixels with alpha > threshold."""
    mask_fg = (alpha0 > 0.5).astype(np.uint8)
    d_ext = cv2.distanceTransform(mask_fg, cv2.DIST_L2, 5)
    exterior_band = (d_ext > 0) & (d_ext < 8)  # exterior within 8px
    if exterior_band.sum() == 0:
        return 0.0
    return float((alpha[exterior_band] > threshold).mean())
```

### Foreground Thinning Detection

Compare mean alpha in the **interior** narrow band between the NPES result and the v2 baseline:

```python
def measure_fg_thinning(alpha_npes, alpha_v2, alpha0, max_acceptable_loss=0.02):
    """Mean alpha loss on interior side. Flag if > max_acceptable_loss."""
    mask_fg = (alpha0 > 0.5).astype(np.uint8)
    mask_bg = 1 - mask_fg
    d_int = cv2.distanceTransform(mask_bg, cv2.DIST_L2, 5)
    interior_band = (d_int > 0) & (d_int < 6)
    if interior_band.sum() == 0:
        return 0.0
    loss = float((alpha_v2[interior_band] - alpha_npes[interior_band]).mean())
    return loss  # positive = thinning; flag if > max_acceptable_loss
```

### Temporal Ghosting Detection

Measure alpha residual in the far exterior (beyond the narrow band) where alpha should be exactly zero:

```python
def measure_temporal_ghosting(alpha, alpha0, far_threshold=12):
    """Mean alpha beyond far_threshold pixels from boundary."""
    mask_fg = (alpha0 > 0.5).astype(np.uint8)
    d_ext = cv2.distanceTransform(mask_fg, cv2.DIST_L2, 5)
    far_exterior = d_ext > far_threshold
    if far_exterior.sum() == 0:
        return 0.0
    return float(alpha[far_exterior].mean())
```

### Chatter Relapse Detection

Use the existing `chatter` metric. Additionally, compute a **per-region chatter** restricted to the exterior band to detect if the sink introduces new instability:

```python
def measure_exterior_chatter(alpha_seq, alpha0_seq, band_width=6):
    """Frame-to-frame alpha variance in the exterior narrow band."""
    diffs = []
    for t in range(1, len(alpha_seq)):
        mask_fg = (alpha0_seq[t] > 0.5).astype(np.uint8)
        d_ext = cv2.distanceTransform(mask_fg, cv2.DIST_L2, 5)
        ext_band = (d_ext > 0) & (d_ext < band_width)
        if ext_band.sum() == 0:
            continue
        diff = np.abs(alpha_seq[t][ext_band] - alpha_seq[t-1][ext_band])
        diffs.append(diff.mean())
    return float(np.mean(diffs)) if diffs else 0.0
```

### Automatic pass/fail gates:

| Metric | PASS | WARN | FAIL |
|--------|------|------|------|
| border_bleed | ≤ 0.0065 | 0.0065–0.0075 | > 0.0075 |
| fg_thinning | ≤ 0.010 | 0.010–0.020 | > 0.020 |
| chatter | ≤ 0.0035 | 0.0035–0.0050 | > 0.0050 |
| temporal_jitter | ≤ 0.0270 | 0.0270–0.0285 | > 0.0285 |
| temporal_ghosting | ≤ 0.001 | 0.001–0.005 | > 0.005 |
| mean_alpha | ≥ 0.430 | 0.425–0.430 | < 0.425 |

## 12. Patch Sketch

### `alpha_fix/config.py` additions

```python
# --- NPES: Normal-Projected Exterior Sink ---
NPES_ENABLED: bool = True
NPES_LAMBDA_S: float = 0.45       # Sink strength [0.0 - 1.0]
NPES_SIGMA_D: float = 2.5         # Signed-distance falloff scale (pixels)
NPES_BETA: float = 2.0            # Background-distance gating exponent
NPES_GAMMA: float = 0.15          # Confidence floor for sink activation
NPES_BAND_ONLY: bool = True       # Restrict to narrow band for performance
```

### `alpha_fix/pipeline.py` additions

```python
# ---------- NPES helper ----------

def _compute_signed_distance(alpha0: np.ndarray) -> np.ndarray:
    """Signed distance field from alpha0 boundary. Positive = exterior."""
    mask_fg = (alpha0 > 0.5).astype(np.uint8)
    mask_bg = (1 - mask_fg).astype(np.uint8)
    # Distance of interior pixels to boundary
    d_interior = cv2.distanceTransform(mask_bg, cv2.DIST_L2, 5).astype(np.float32)
    # Distance of exterior pixels to boundary
    d_exterior = cv2.distanceTransform(mask_fg, cv2.DIST_L2, 5).astype(np.float32)
    # Combine: negative inside, positive outside
    signed_d = np.where(mask_fg > 0, -d_interior, d_exterior)
    return signed_d


def apply_npes(
    alpha: np.ndarray,
    alpha0: np.ndarray,
    b_lab: np.ndarray,
    confidence: np.ndarray,
    band_mask: np.ndarray | None = None,
    lambda_s: float = 0.45,
    sigma_d: float = 2.5,
    beta: float = 2.0,
    gamma: float = 0.15,
) -> np.ndarray:
    """
    Normal-Projected Exterior Sink.
    Reduces alpha on the exterior (background) side of the boundary
    without affecting the interior (foreground) side.
    """
    signed_d = _compute_signed_distance(alpha0)

    # Exterior projection
    e = np.maximum(signed_d, 0.0)

    # Sink potential: Gaussian complement
    S = 1.0 - np.exp(-(e ** 2) / (2.0 * sigma_d ** 2))

    # Background-distance gate
    G = np.power(np.clip(1.0 - b_lab, 0.0, 1.0), beta)

    # Confidence gate
    denom = max(1.0 - gamma, 1e-8)
    W_C = np.clip((confidence - gamma) / denom, 0.0, 1.0)

    # Correction (always non-positive)
    delta = -lambda_s * S * G * W_C * alpha

    # Apply with optional band restriction
    if band_mask is not None:
        result = alpha.copy()
        bp = band_mask > 0
        result[bp] = np.clip(alpha[bp] + delta[bp], 0.0, 1.0)
        return result
    else:
        return np.clip(alpha + delta, 0.0, 1.0)
```

### Insertion in the main processing loop

```python
# In the per-frame processing function, after temporal EMA:

    # ... existing code ...
    alpha = apply_temporal_ema(alpha, alpha_prev_warped, ...)
    
    # ──► NEW: NPES exterior sink correction
    if config.NPES_ENABLED:
        alpha = apply_npes(
            alpha=alpha,
            alpha0=alpha0,
            b_lab=b_lab_normalized,
            confidence=confidence_map,
            band_mask=band_mask if config.NPES_BAND_ONLY else None,
            lambda_s=config.NPES_LAMBDA_S,
            sigma_d=config.NPES_SIGMA_D,
            beta=config.NPES_BETA,
            gamma=config.NPES_GAMMA,
        )
    
    # ... existing edge cleanup / despill ...
    alpha = apply_edge_cleanup(alpha, ...)
```

## Relationship to Known Methods

NPES draws on two classical ideas, combined in a novel way specific to the Alpha Fix pipeline:

- **Narrow-band level-set methods** use signed distance fields and restrict computation to a band around the zero level set. NPES uses signed distance similarly but as a gating signal for a multiplicative alpha correction rather than as a contour evolution PDE.[8][7]
- **Anisotropic diffusion (Perona-Malik)** modulates diffusion strength based on local image gradients. NPES's background-distance gate \(G(p)\) serves an analogous role — modulating correction strength based on color evidence — but operates as a one-shot multiplicative correction rather than an iterative diffusion process.[9][10]

The specific combination — one-sided signed-distance gating × background-color evidence × confidence floor × multiplicative alpha sink — is novel to this pipeline and does not correspond to a named method in the matting or level-set literature.

The full NPES method design is in the attached report. Here's the short version:

**Normal-Projected Exterior Sink (NPES)** is a single-pass, closed-form local correction that inserts after your temporal EMA step. It computes the signed distance from the α₀ boundary, takes the positive part (exterior only), and applies a multiplicative alpha reduction gated by three signals: a Gaussian-complement distance profile (stronger further outside), a background-color gate using your existing LAB distance (protects foreground-colored pixels), and a confidence floor (preserves anti-chatter in ambiguous zones).

The report covers all 12 deliverables: the exact diagnosis of why v2 bleeds (symmetric EMA locks in exterior leakage), the full math with every symbol defined, why it's zero on the interior side, why it preserves chatter and jitter, complete pseudocode, exact pipeline insertion point, a 5-pass ablation plan with concrete parameter values, automatic failure metrics with pass/warn/fail gates, and a copy-paste patch sketch for `pipeline.py` and `config.py`.

# 🧪 BISTABLE ALPHA REACTOR (BAR)

## The Insane Premise

Treat the alpha matte as a **chemical concentration field** and run a modified Allen-Cahn reaction-diffusion PDE on it for a handful of micro-iterations per frame. The reaction term is a bistable double-well potential that naturally drives alpha toward either 0 or 1, forming sharp interfaces between the two stable states. The diffusion term is anisotropic and asymmetric — suppressed on the interior, active on the exterior — so the sharpening only eats the bleed side.

This is genuinely unhinged. Nobody runs reaction-diffusion dynamics on alpha mattes. But the math is sound: the Allen-Cahn equation is the canonical PDE for producing sharp phase boundaries from a diffuse initial condition. Bistable reaction-diffusion systems naturally form sharp, stable interfaces between two rest states. The entire point of the equation is to take a mushy transition zone and evolve it into a crisp sigmoid — which is exactly the transition-zone shaping problem that NPES, PairedShellTransport, and DCMT all failed to solve.[1][2][3][4]

## Why This Is Not As Crazy As It Sounds

The pass_15 problem is: the alpha transition profile is too gradual on the exterior side. Every method tried so far has been a **single-pass correction** — multiply by some factor, subtract some amount, move some mass. They all modify alpha values but don't reshape the profile's functional form.

The Allen-Cahn equation does something fundamentally different: it evolves the profile toward a **canonical sigmoid shape** dictated by the double-well potential. The steady-state solution of the 1D Allen-Cahn equation is exactly \(\alpha(x) = \frac{1}{2}(1 - \tanh(x / (2\varepsilon)))\), a perfectly symmetric sigmoid whose width is controlled by \(\varepsilon\). By running a few micro-iterations, the current mushy exterior profile gets pulled toward this target shape — not by an ad-hoc correction factor, but by a PDE whose fixed points ARE sharp transitions.[1]

## Mathematical Formulation

### The Standard Allen-Cahn Equation

\[ \frac{\partial \alpha}{\partial t} = \varepsilon^2 \nabla^2 \alpha - W'(\alpha) \]

[5]

where \(W(\alpha) = \alpha^2(1-\alpha)^2\) is the double-well potential with minima at \(\alpha = 0\) and \(\alpha = 1\), and \(W'(\alpha) = 2\alpha(1-\alpha)(2\alpha - 1)\) is its derivative. The term \(-W'(\alpha)\) is the **reaction**: it pushes alpha toward 0 or 1 depending on which side of 0.5 it's on. The term \(\varepsilon^2 \nabla^2 \alpha\) is the **diffusion**: it smooths the interface to prevent infinite sharpness.[1]

### The BAR Modifications

The standard Allen-Cahn would sharpen both sides equally — eating into the foreground just as much as cleaning the background. That would be terrible. BAR applies four modifications:

#### Modification 1: Asymmetric Reaction via Tilted Double-Well

Tilt the potential so that \(\alpha = 0\) is a deeper well than \(\alpha = 1\):

\[ W_{\text{tilt}}(\alpha) = \alpha^2(1-\alpha)^2 - \eta \cdot \alpha \cdot h(d) \]

[6]

where \(\eta\) is the tilt strength and \(h(d)\) is a signed-distance gate:

\[ h(d) = \text{clamp}\!\left(\frac{d}{R},\; 0,\; 1\right) \]

[7]

Here \(d\) is signed distance (positive = exterior, your convention). On the interior (\(d \leq 0\)), \(h = 0\) and the potential is symmetric — no bias. On the exterior, the well at \(\alpha = 0\) gets deeper, creating a thermodynamic bias toward zero. Alpha on the exterior side gets a stronger "gravity pull" toward background.

The derivative of the tilted potential:

\[ W_{\text{tilt}}'(\alpha) = 2\alpha(1-\alpha)(2\alpha - 1) - \eta \cdot h(d) \]

[8]

#### Modification 2: Anisotropic Diffusion Suppression

Replace isotropic diffusion with direction-gated diffusion. Suppress diffusion in the direction of the boundary normal on the interior side (prevent smoothing that would thin the foreground), allow it on the exterior side:

\[ D(p) = \varepsilon^2 \cdot \left(1 - \lambda_D \cdot \max(0, -d(p)/R)\right) \]

[9]

On the exterior (\(d > 0\)): \(D = \varepsilon^2\) — full diffusion, allowing the reaction to sharpen freely. On the deep interior (\(d \ll 0\)): \(D \to \varepsilon^2(1 - \lambda_D)\) — suppressed diffusion, protecting existing foreground alpha from being smoothed away. The parameter \(\lambda_D \in [0, 0.9]\) controls how much interior diffusion is suppressed.

#### Modification 3: Confidence-Gated Time Step

Scale the effective time step by the confidence map to avoid destabilizing ambiguous regions:

\[ \Delta t_{\text{eff}}(p) = \Delta t \cdot W_C(p) \]

[10]

where \(W_C(p) = \text{clamp}((C(p) - \gamma_c) / (1 - \gamma_c), 0, 1)\). In low-confidence zones, the PDE effectively doesn't run — preserving the EMA's anti-chatter work.

#### Modification 4: Anchor Pinning

Pin alpha at pixels where the anchor \(\alpha_0\) is strongly definite (clearly 0 or clearly 1):

\[ \alpha^{n+1}(p) = \begin{cases} \alpha_0(p) & \text{if } |d(p)| > R_{\text{pin}} \\ \text{PDE update} & \text{otherwise} \end{cases} \]

[11]

This prevents the reaction-diffusion from eating into deep foreground or creating spurious activation in deep background. The PDE only runs inside the narrow band.

### The BAR Update Rule

Combining everything, the per-micro-iteration update at each narrow-band pixel:

\[ \alpha^{n+1}(p) = \alpha^n(p) + \Delta t_{\text{eff}}(p) \cdot \left[ D(p) \cdot \nabla^2 \alpha^n(p) - W_{\text{tilt}}'(\alpha^n(p)) \right] \]

[12]

Run this for \(N_{\text{iter}}\) micro-iterations (3–8 is the target range). Each micro-iteration is just:
- One Laplacian convolution (3×3 kernel, OpenCV `filter2D`)
- Elementwise multiply by diffusion coefficient
- Elementwise reaction evaluation
- Elementwise multiply by confidence-gated time step
- Add and clamp

### Symbol Table

| Symbol | Definition | Typical Value |
|--------|-----------|---------------|
| \(\alpha^n(p)\) | Alpha at pixel \(p\) after \(n\) micro-iterations | — |
| \(d(p)\) | Signed distance; positive = exterior | — |
| \(R\) | Band half-width for normalization | 6.0 px |
| \(R_{\text{pin}}\) | Pinning radius beyond which alpha is locked | 8.0 px |
| \(\varepsilon\) | Interface width parameter | 1.0–2.0 |
| \(\eta\) | Exterior tilt strength | 0.05–0.20 |
| \(\lambda_D\) | Interior diffusion suppression | 0.5–0.8 |
| \(\Delta t\) | Micro-iteration time step | 0.1–0.3 |
| \(N_{\text{iter}}\) | Number of micro-iterations per frame | 3–8 |
| \(\gamma_c\) | Confidence floor | 0.12 |
| \(C(p)\) | Confidence map | \([0, 1]\) |
| \(W_C(p)\) | Confidence gate | \([0, 1]\) |

## Why This Might Actually Work

### What it does to the transition profile

The double-well reaction term \(-W'(\alpha) = -2\alpha(1-\alpha)(2\alpha-1)\) has three fixed points: \(\alpha = 0\) (stable), \(\alpha = 0.5\) (unstable), \(\alpha = 1\) (stable). Any alpha value between 0 and 0.5 gets pushed toward 0. Any value between 0.5 and 1 gets pushed toward 1. The exterior tilt shifts the unstable fixed point below 0.5 on the exterior side, meaning alpha values slightly above 0.5 on the exterior get pulled toward 0 instead of 1. This is the mechanism that shrinks the transition zone from the outside.

The diffusion term prevents the reaction from creating a discontinuous step function — it maintains a smooth sigmoid of finite width \(\sim 2\varepsilon\). By choosing \(\varepsilon\) to match the desired transition width (1–2 pixels), the PDE converges toward a transition zone of exactly the right width.[13][1]

### Why previous methods didn't have this

NPES, CGDT, NAPS — all single-pass multiplicative corrections. They can reduce alpha but can't **reshape the profile's functional form**. DCMT used a shock, which is related but uncontrolled — it pushes alpha toward local extrema indiscriminately. The Allen-Cahn reaction is different: it pushes alpha toward **global** attractors (0 and 1) through a smooth potential, with the interface width controlled by the diffusion coefficient.[14]

### Why it might preserve chatter

The confidence gate \(W_C\) shuts the PDE off in ambiguous zones. In high-confidence zones, the PDE drives alpha toward 0 or 1 — which are fixed points. Once alpha reaches a fixed point, the reaction term vanishes and the update is zero. Fixed points don't chatter. The PDE is literally designing for temporal stability by definition: its equilibrium states are the most stable configurations of the energy functional.[3]

### Why it might preserve jitter

The PDE is deterministic, smooth, and its fixed points (0, 1, and the sigmoid interface) are identical across frames. If two consecutive frames start with similar post-EMA alpha fields, a few micro-iterations of the same PDE will produce similar results. The Laplacian diffusion additionally smooths any frame-to-frame noise.

## Why This Might Gloriously Fail

This is the sandbox, so let's be honest about the failure modes:

- **Over-sharpening**: If \(\varepsilon\) is too small or \(N_{\text{iter}}\) too high, the PDE could eat through legitimate soft edges (hair, motion blur, semi-transparent regions) and turn them into hard binary cuts. The matte would look "cut out with scissors."
- **Ringing / instability**: The explicit Euler scheme for Allen-Cahn requires \(\Delta t < \varepsilon^2 / 2\) for stability in 2D. If the time step is too large, oscillations appear. The fix is easy (reduce \(\Delta t\)) but the range of stable parameters might be narrow.[1]
- **Reaction-diffusion patterns**: If the parameters are badly chosen, Allen-Cahn on a 2D domain can produce Turing-like patterns — spots, stripes, labyrinths. This would be catastrophic for alpha quality. The mitigation is anchor pinning + few iterations + confidence gating, but in pathological cases (very noisy alpha input), spurious patterns could nucleate.[15][4]
- **Slow**: 3–8 Laplacian convolutions per frame per iteration. Probably 2–5ms extra per frame. Might be fine. Might not.

## Pseudocode

```python
def apply_bar(alpha, alpha0, signed_d, confidence, band_mask,
              epsilon=1.5, eta=0.10, lambda_D=0.6, dt=0.15,
              n_iter=5, R=6.0, R_pin=8.0, gamma_c=0.12):
    """
    Bistable Alpha Reactor (BAR).
    Runs a modified Allen-Cahn reaction-diffusion PDE on the alpha matte.
    """
    # Laplacian kernel
    lapl_kernel = np.array([[0, 1, 0],
                            [1,-4, 1],
                            [0, 1, 0]], dtype=np.float32)

    # Precompute spatial maps (constant across micro-iterations)
    # Exterior tilt gate
    h = np.clip(signed_d / R, 0.0, 1.0)

    # Anisotropic diffusion coefficient
    interior_supp = np.clip(-signed_d / R, 0.0, 1.0)
    D_map = epsilon**2 * (1.0 - lambda_D * interior_supp)

    # Confidence gate on time step
    W_C = np.clip((confidence - gamma_c) / max(1.0 - gamma_c, 1e-8), 0.0, 1.0)
    dt_eff = dt * W_C

    # Pinning mask: True where PDE should NOT run
    pin_mask = np.abs(signed_d) > R_pin

    # Band restriction
    active = (band_mask > 0) & (~pin_mask)

    # Run micro-iterations
    a = alpha.copy()
    for _ in range(n_iter):
        # Laplacian via convolution
        lap_a = cv2.filter2D(a, cv2.CV_32F, lapl_kernel)

        # Reaction: tilted double-well derivative
        # W'(a) = 2a(1-a)(2a-1) - eta * h
        reaction = 2.0 * a * (1.0 - a) * (2.0 * a - 1.0) - eta * h

        # Full update
        update = D_map * lap_a - reaction

        # Apply with confidence-gated time step, only in active band
        a[active] = np.clip(
            a[active] + dt_eff[active] * update[active],
            0.0, 1.0
        )

    # Re-pin exterior and interior
    a[pin_mask] = alpha[pin_mask]

    return a
```

## Insertion Point

```
... temporal EMA ...
──► apply_bar()       ← HERE: REPLACES or FOLLOWS any NAPS/CGDT step
... edge cleanup / despill ...
```

In the sandbox, run BAR **instead of** NAPS to see what the reaction-diffusion does on its own. If it shows promise, a later experiment could run NAPS first (gentle profile steepening) then BAR (PDE-polish to sigmoid).
